<div id="review_151870426" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/151870426" />
    <a title="Arlene" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1746727-arlene"><img alt="Arlene" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1347048609p2/1746727.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/151870426?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Arlene" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Arlene" href="/user/show/1746727-arlene">Arlene</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780385533225" href="/book/show/8336984-the-particular-sadness-of-lemon-cake">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">GoodReads Giveaway Winner</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1746727-arlene?shelf=awful">awful</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1746727-arlene?shelf=read-2011">read-2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer151870426" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2635523287365450168">~jumps up and down~<br/>I finished! I finished! Good Gawd I finished the book!!<br/><br/><b>A Particular Sadness for Lemon Cake</b> is a combination of unique, disturbing and unorganized. I have no other words to describe this novel as I found myself tuning in and out of the events as they were slapped together in a messy sort of way that made me feel tossed around from scene to scene in a random fashion. <br/><br/>First off, it took me a few chapters to even achieve mild interest, but then when Part II came along all the way </span>
  <span id="freeText2635523287365450168" style="display:none">~jumps up and down~<br />I finished! I finished! Good Gawd I finished the book!!<br /><br /><b>A Particular Sadness for Lemon Cake</b> is a combination of unique, disturbing and unorganized. I have no other words to describe this novel as I found myself tuning in and out of the events as they were slapped together in a messy sort of way that made me feel tossed around from scene to scene in a random fashion. <br /><br />First off, it took me a few chapters to even achieve mild interest, but then when Part II came along all the way to the conclusion, I was completely lost and confused with the shift in the story’s theme. Classic examples of an author who is unable to carry her original story concept all the way through the book. Bummer…<br /><br />I understand developing a novel around a girl’s ability to taste the emotions of people through food is a rather limited concept to develop, so this book would have been better served as a short story without the bizarre events involving Rose, Joseph, her mother and father and all sorts of nonsensical happenings that served as wasted fluff.<br /><br />When the story commences, Rose is turning nine years old and she realizes upon eating a lemon cake her mom made for her birthday that she can sense the sadness her mother was experiencing while baking the cake. From then on, she takes a careful approach to eating as a full gamut of emotions can be conveyed to her from her brother’s frustration when she nibbles on his toast to the despair her mother is feeling when she eats the dinner that was prepared. <br /><br />In my opinion, nine year old Rose is a too young of a narrator to carry the first part of the story. I say that because I have a feeling that the author truly underestimates a child’s ability to communicate at that age and it just resulted in frustration for the reader… in my case at least. I know because I have nieces and nephews that are quite verbose in expressing their feelings and let’s just say they can come across loud and clear. I was extremely frustrated when Rose was unable to explain to her mother that the food she eats drives all sorts of feelings in her, and I wanted to shake the mother and say, “Can’t you see when she eats your pot roast she becomes upset because she knows you’re having an affair!?!’ Gah! So the voice of the MC is all wrong IMHO for the first 120 pages or so, or possibly just poorly executed. <br /><br />Then when the setting of the story shifts to seventeen year old Rose, it takes a turn down bizarre avenue. I suspected all along that her brother Joseph had some form of autism, and I was leaning toward Asperger syndrome. Okay I know… I’m no doctor, but his lack of interest with social interaction and focused, repetitive behaviors and interests led me down that path. I wish Aimee Bender would have worked that angle rather than play out that bizarre scene with Joseph and the chair in his apartment and his multiple disappearances. What a hot mess and a ginormous WTF! A CHAIR!!! Holy hell, I won’t even waste my time contemplating what that was all about. <br /><br />Another thing that really bugged me… Rose’s mother is having an affair and Rose figures it out at the age of 12. So, she tells her daughter she’ll stop the affair if Rose wants her too… errr… not because it’s wrong…. errr….. not because she feels bad about it… but only if Rose tells her to stop, which BTW Rose tells her to carry on with her love fest. Someone, please tell me I’m not wrong in fuming about this idiotic situation… Finally… FINALLLY when the story mercifully comes to a close and I believe Rose is now around twenty-two, I can’t say it’s wrapped up with much success because I was just left with a feeling that I wasted my time with this book and that’s always a disappointing experience.<br /><br />One more thing… <i>“I’m fine… Just fine.”</i> Someone please hit me upside the head if I ever say those words in that order ever again…<br /><br />So finally, let me try to extrapolate at least one thing I gleaned from <b>The Particular Sadness of Lemon Cake</b>, I did find myself wondering if my relationship with my family and friends would be different if I could truly know what they are feeling as many of us keep our honest emotions hidden deep down. Overall though, I didn’t care for this one for many reasons... </span>
  <a data-text-id="2635523287365450168" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer452231262203755796">when i first met aimee bender in a writing class at ucsd, she was the most magical yet misunderstood writer in the room. over two decades later, it's nice to see that nothing has changed. she is consistently original. i'd say that she writes like a lighter, equally offbeat, miniature version of tom robbins. except i don't really like robbins and i sure do like bender. go, aimee bender! go, ucsd!</span>

<div id="review_153446327" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/153446327" />
    <a title="Nancy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/113545-nancy"><img alt="Nancy" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/153446327?book_show_action=true">Mar 10, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nancy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nancy" href="/user/show/113545-nancy">Nancy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer153446327" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17729378287223494420">Sometimes I really, really want to like a book. I really wanted to love "The Particular Sadness of Lemon Cake," because the lovely Sherry H so kindly sent it to me. Thanks, Sherry! That was so nice of you!<br/><br/>My initial resistance to this novel was that I thought it would be too twee, too frothily chick-littish, and it was neither of those things. Bender is an eloquent writer with a gift for stripping her characters down to their quivering nerve-ends. Unfortunately for me, though, this novel falls s</span>
  <span id="freeText17729378287223494420" style="display:none">Sometimes I really, really want to like a book. I really wanted to love "The Particular Sadness of Lemon Cake," because the lovely Sherry H so kindly sent it to me. Thanks, Sherry! That was so nice of you!<br /><br />My initial resistance to this novel was that I thought it would be too twee, too frothily chick-littish, and it was neither of those things. Bender is an eloquent writer with a gift for stripping her characters down to their quivering nerve-ends. Unfortunately for me, though, this novel falls solidly into the genre of magical realism, and I just couldn't buy in to the magical central concept. The narrator, Rose, discovers at the tender age of nine that the food she eats tastes of the emotional state of the people who prepared it...and of the factories, transport, and farmers that produce it. This is made truly frightful by the distraught state her mother is always in; Rose's mother Lane feels depressed, worthless, and empty most of the time, and little Rose literally cannot stomach this barren emotional diet. <br /><br />I think I could have been down with the book if Bender had limited the magic to Rose and her eating disorder; I liked how she didn't flinch from the extreme unpleasantness that ensued, and I liked the concept as a metaphor for the way that children must endure the pain of their parents' wounds. But as the book proceeds, Rose discovers that other family members have "special talents," and I found these absurd...especially her brother's "talent," which made me giggle. And since giggling was NOT the aimed-at reaction, I think I will say: "Lemon Cake" got it only about half right for me; maybe I will like a future book by Bender better.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17729378287223494420" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_142573273" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/142573273" />
    <a title="trina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/910324-trina"><img alt="trina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581483418p2/910324.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/142573273?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="trina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="trina" href="/user/show/910324-trina">trina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">absolutely no one</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/910324-trina?shelf=over-rated-and-terrible">over-rated-and-terrible</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer142573273" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6124027495939757182">for having such a fantastic and intriguing premise, this book sure is completely godawful. being a fan of magic realism, and of 'like water for chocolate', another book about emotion informing food and the course of an entire family's existence, i thought for sure i'd enjoy a book about a little girl who can taste people's emotions by eating the food they've prepared and how she copes with learning her loved one's deepest secrets... i was wrong. or at least i pinned my hopes on the wrong book. i</span>
  <span id="freeText6124027495939757182" style="display:none">for having such a fantastic and intriguing premise, this book sure is completely godawful. being a fan of magic realism, and of 'like water for chocolate', another book about emotion informing food and the course of an entire family's existence, i thought for sure i'd enjoy a book about a little girl who can taste people's emotions by eating the food they've prepared and how she copes with learning her loved one's deepest secrets... i was wrong. or at least i pinned my hopes on the wrong book. in someone else's hands, it might've been masterful. <br /><br />i've read so much praise of this aimee bender person, i was utterly prepared to love this book, and then it just... shit all over my expectations. her prose is overwritten, i see nothing lyrical about it. her characters are flat little convenient cliches of dysfunctional family members, and for the most part totally unlikable and unsympathetic. you'd think, with our main character being able to discern secret feelings and longings, that there'd be more character development than 'mom was sad for no apparent reason and now she's having an affair' and 'dad is very logical and distant aka a lawyer'. AND THEN to add insult to injury, there's 'my brother was a genius and then he turned into a chair.' really??? is it supposed to be allegorical? are there real people in the world who are this awkward and disconnected and totally not-human? i understand its nitpicky, when the book involves certain magical elements, to expect the characters to act according to the rules of our real world... only it's not nitpicky at all. the book takes place in the real world, only the people are utterly one-dimensional. their stupid magical 'gifts' are far more believable than their personalities (or utter lack thereof). our main character, her thoughts are never the believable thoughts of a child of eight, or thirteen, or seventeen. nor are her actions anything other than robotic and convenient and milquetoasty. AND to make matters worse, the story goes nowhere, no one learns anything, nothing is resolved... you finish this book only to wish you'd never picked it up to begin with. i read it in one sitting, knowing that if i put it down, i'd never pick it up again, hoping it would pick up, begin to go somewhere, only to be dejected and sleep deprived two hundred pages later. hats off to you, aimee bender, for getting this pap not only published but praised. you inspire me to start writing again, if only because i could probably do a better job. in my sleep.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6124027495939757182" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14258479190701846744">One and a half stars.<br/>Rose Edelstein discovers she has acquired a unique talent.   <span id="freeText14258479190701846744" style="display:none">One and a half stars.<br />Rose Edelstein discovers she has acquired a unique talent. She can taste the emotion of her mother in the lemon cholate cake she makes for her birthday. But it is not a good feeling. She tastes her mother’s despair. From that point on Rose foods she can tell the feelings or emotions of the makers of other foods when tasted. Whether the people are, angry, lonely, frustrated or whatever other emotion they experience, Rose can taste it. Things people think are hidden are brought to light. Her brother Joseph who disappears at times, also has a special ability that is revealed later in the book. <br />The premise of this story was interesting enough to get me in. The pacing moves along at a slowish pace, but the writing still maintained interest. However I did find the 'eyes floating around the room,' presented a very strange picture. But generally the writing was interesting enough as more of this family’s secrets are exposed. After a while though it tended to get a bit bogged down and I was starting to get over the whole taste and feel thing, but wanted to see how it all ended up. However once I got to the incident with Joseph and the chair, I started to rapidly lose interest. I did persist to the end but then when I came to the end was left thinking, what was the point of it all? <br />Those who like surreal books may enjoy this, as I did for a while. Readers who object to no quotation marks will not appreciate this book for that reason. But it was the weirdness of the story that never seemed resolved which lost me. Probably I am just not the right reader for this author’s work.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14258479190701846744" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('PQ7asN6UIn+y//0Wb45VckQP6L2533U4Bl9M7I0cS05I/Vf5hCQwV4XPnlXCYlKWK3I2nTeWqLuExfkpzqwO9A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  }

La sua specializzazione è l\'ipnosi e i suoi pazienti hanno una cosa in comune: sono bambini. Spesso traumatizzati, segnati da eventi drammatici o in possesso di informazioni importanti sepolte nella loro fragile memoria, di cui polizia e magistrati si servono per le indagini. Pietro è il migliore di tutta Firenze, dove è conosciuto come l\'addormentatore di bambini. Ma quando riceve una telefonata dall\'altro capo del mondo da parte di una collega australiana che gli raccomanda una paziente, Pietro reagisce con perplessità e diffidenza. Perché Hanna Hall è un\'adulta. Hanna è tormentata da un ricordo vivido, ma che potrebbe non essere reale: un omicidio. E per capire se quel frammento di memoria corrisponde alla verità o è un\'illusione, ha disperato bisogno di Pietro Gerber. Hanna è un\'adulta oggi, ma quel ricordo risale alla sua infanzia. E Pietro dovrà aiutarla a far riemergere la bambina che è ancora dentro di lei. Una bambina dai molti nomi, tenuta sempre lontana dagli estranei e che, con la sua famiglia, viveva felice in un luogo incantato: la «casa delle voci». Quella bambina, a dieci anni, ha assistito a un omicidio. O forse non ha semplicemente visto. Forse l\'assassina è proprio lei.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"11197606683124816965\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div Si rifiuta di obbedire automaticamente ai riti cui sembra sottostare l’umanità: trovare un lavoro, farsi una famiglia… la sua vita è attratta piuttosto dal gorgo del vuoto, e da quattro anni è in caduta “precisa come un tuffo da olimpionico”. Non ha più nemmeno la forza di scrivere, e la sua esistenza sembra priva di uno scopo. È per i suoi genitori che Daniele prova a chiedere aiuto, deve riuscire a sopravvivere, lo farà attraverso il lavoro. Il 3 marzo del 1999 firma un contratto con una cooperativa legata all’ospedale pediatrico Bambino Gesù di Roma.\nIn questa “casa” speciale, abitata dai bambini segnati dalla malattia, sono molti gli sguardi che incontra e che via via lo spingeranno a porsi una domanda scomoda: perché, se la sofferenza pare essere l’unica legge che governa il mondo, vale comunque la pena di vivere e provare a costruire qualcosa?\nLe risposte arriveranno, al di là di qualsiasi retorica e con deflagrante potenza, dall’esperienza quotidiana di fatica e solidarietà tra compagni di lavoro, in un luogo come il Bambino Gesù, in cui l’essenza della vita si mostra in tutta la sua brutalità e negli squarci di inattesa bellezza. Qui Daniele sentirà dentro di sé un invito sempre più imperioso a non chiudere gli occhi, e lo accoglierà come un dono.\n\nCon la lingua precisa e affilata del poeta, Daniele Mencarelli ci offre con grazia cruda il racconto coraggioso del rifugio cercato nell’alcol, della spirale di solitudine, prostrazione e vergogna di quegli anni bui, e della progressiva liberazione dalla sofferenza fino alla Con una madre troppo occupata nel lavoro e un padre che sta affrontando una crisi di coppia senza fine, Giada sembra in pace con se stessa solo in palestra, dove il maestro De Roma – un ex savateur dai trascorsi drammatici e un passato poco limpido – la prepara al suo primo combattimento.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/5285.Aimee_Bender">About Aimee Bender</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/5285.Aimee_Bender"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1291155858p3/5285.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7231197272005021761"><b> Aimee Bender </b> is the author of the novel <i>An Invisible Sign of My Own</i> and of the collections <i>The Girl in the Flammable Skirt</i> and <i>Willful Creatures</i>. Her work has been widely anthologized and has been translated into ten languages. She lives in Los Angeles.</span>

</div>
